[←11. The Conversion Tactic Mode](The-Conversion-Tactic-Mode/#conv "11. The Conversion Tactic Mode")
#  12. Axioms and Computation[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=axioms-and-computation "Permalink")
We have seen that the version of the Calculus of Constructions that has been implemented in Lean includes dependent function types, inductive types, and a hierarchy of universes that starts with an [impredicative](Quantifiers-and-Equality/#--tech-term-impredicative), [proof-irrelevant](Propositions-and-Proofs/#--tech-term-proof-irrelevance) `Prop` at the bottom. In this chapter, we consider ways of extending the CIC with additional axioms and rules. Extending a foundational system in such a way is often convenient; it can make it possible to prove more theorems, as well as make it easier to prove theorems that could have been proved otherwise. But there can be negative consequences of adding additional axioms, consequences which may go beyond concerns about their correctness. In particular, the use of axioms bears on the computational content of definitions and theorems, in ways we will explore here.
Lean is designed to support both computational and classical reasoning. Users that are so inclined can stick to a “computationally pure” fragment, which guarantees that closed expressions in the system evaluate to canonical normal forms. In particular, any closed computationally pure expression of type `[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")`, for example, will reduce to a numeral.
Lean's standard library defines an additional axiom, propositional extensionality, and a quotient construction which in turn implies the principle of function extensionality. These extensions are used, for example, to develop theories of sets and finite sets. We will see below that using these theorems can block evaluation in Lean's kernel, so that closed terms of type `[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")` no longer evaluate to numerals. But Lean erases types and propositional information when compiling definitions to executable code, and since these axioms only add new propositions, they are compatible with that computational interpretation. Even computationally inclined users may wish to use the classical law of the excluded middle to reason about computation. This also blocks evaluation in the kernel, but it is compatible with compiled code.
The standard library also defines a choice principle that is entirely antithetical to a computational interpretation, since it magically produces “data” from a proposition asserting its existence. Its use is essential to some classical constructions, and users can import it when needed. But expressions that use this construction to produce data do not have computational content, and in Lean we are required to mark such definitions as `noncomputable` to flag that fact.
Using a clever trick (known as Diaconescu's theorem), one can use propositional extensionality, function extensionality, and choice to derive the law of the excluded middle. As noted above, however, use of the law of the excluded middle is still compatible with compilation, as are other classical principles, as long as they are not used to manufacture data.
To summarize, then, on top of the underlying framework of universes, dependent function types, and inductive types, the standard library adds three additional components:
  * the axiom of propositional extensionality
  * a quotient construction, which implies function extensionality
  * a choice principle, which produces data from an existential proposition.


The first two of these block normalization within Lean, but are compatible with code generation, whereas the third is not amenable to computational interpretation. We will spell out the details more precisely below.
##  12.1. Historical and Philosophical Context[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=historical-and-philosophical-context "Permalink")
For most of its history, mathematics was essentially computational: geometry dealt with constructions of geometric objects, algebra was concerned with algorithmic solutions to systems of equations, and analysis provided means to compute the future behavior of systems evolving over time. From the proof of a theorem to the effect that “for every `x`, there is a `y` such that ...”, it was generally straightforward to extract an algorithm to compute such a `y` given `x`.
In the nineteenth century, however, increases in the complexity of mathematical arguments pushed mathematicians to develop new styles of reasoning that suppress algorithmic information and invoke descriptions of mathematical objects that abstract away the details of how those objects are represented. The goal was to obtain a powerful “conceptual” understanding without getting bogged down in computational details, but this had the effect of admitting mathematical theorems that are simply _false_ on a direct computational reading.
There is still fairly uniform agreement today that computation is important to mathematics. But there are different views as to how best to address computational concerns. From a _constructive_ point of view, it is a mistake to separate mathematics from its computational roots; every meaningful mathematical theorem should have a direct computational interpretation. From a _classical_ point of view, it is more fruitful to maintain a separation of concerns: we can use one language and body of methods to write computer programs, while maintaining the freedom to use nonconstructive theories and methods to reason about them. Lean is designed to support both of these approaches. Core parts of the library are developed constructively, but the system also provides support for carrying out classical mathematical reasoning.
Computationally, the purest part of dependent type theory avoids the use of `Prop` entirely. Inductive types and dependent function types can be viewed as data types, and terms of these types can be “evaluated” by applying reduction rules until no more rules can be applied. In principle, any closed term (that is, term with no free variables) of type `[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")` should evaluate to a numeral, `[succ](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat.succ in Lean Language Reference") (… ([succ](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat.succ in Lean Language Reference") [zero](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat.zero in Lean Language Reference"))…)`.
Introducing a proof-irrelevant `Prop` and marking theorems irreducible represents a first step towards separation of concerns. The intention is that elements of a type `p : Prop` should play no role in computation, and so the particular construction of a term `prf : p` is “irrelevant” in that sense. One can still define computational objects that incorporate elements of type `Prop`; the point is that these elements can help us reason about the effects of the computation, but can be ignored when we extract “code” from the term. Elements of type `Prop` are not entirely innocuous, however. They include equations `s = t : α` for any type `α`, and such equations can be used as casts, to type check terms. Below, we will see examples of how such casts can block computation in the system. However, computation is still possible under an evaluation scheme that erases propositional content, ignores intermediate typing constraints, and reduces terms until they reach a normal form. This is precisely what Lean's virtual machine does.
Having adopted a proof-irrelevant `Prop`, one might consider it legitimate to use, for example, the law of the excluded middle, `p ∨ ¬p`, where `p` is any proposition. Of course, this, too, can block computation according to the rules of CIC, but it does not prevent the generation of executable code, as described above. It is only the choice principles discussed in [the section on choice](Axioms-and-Computation/#choice) that completely erase the distinction between the proof-irrelevant and data-relevant parts of the theory.
##  12.2. Propositional Extensionality[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=propositional-extensionality "Permalink")
Propositional extensionality is the following axiom:
`[axiom](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Axioms/#Lean___Parser___Command___axiom-next "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") propext {a b : Prop} : (a ↔ b) → a = b `
It asserts that when two propositions imply one another, they are actually equal. This is consistent with set-theoretic interpretations in which any element `a : Prop` is either empty or the singleton set `{∗}\{\ast\}{∗}`, for some distinguished element `∗\ast∗`. The axiom has the effect that equivalent propositions can be substituted for one another in any context:
`[variable](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___variable "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") (a b c d e : Prop)  ``theorem thm₁ (h : a ↔ b) : (c ∧ a ∧ d → e) ↔ (c ∧ b ∧ d → e) :=   [propext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Propositions/#propext "Documentation for propext in Lean Language Reference") h ▸ Iff.refl _  ``theorem thm₂ (p : Prop → Prop) (h : a ↔ b) (h₁ : p a) : p b :=   [propext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Propositions/#propext "Documentation for propext in Lean Language Reference") h ▸ h₁ ```
##  12.3. Function Extensionality[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=function-extensionality "Permalink")
Similar to propositional extensionality, function extensionality asserts that any two functions of type `(x : α) → β x` that agree on all their inputs are equal:
`[funext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Functions/#funext "Documentation for funext in Lean Language Reference").{u, v}   {α : Sort u} {β : α → Sort v}   {f g : (x : α) → β x}   (h : ∀ (x : α), f x = g x) :   f = g `
From a classical, set-theoretic perspective, this is exactly what it means for two functions to be equal. This is known as an “extensional” view of functions. From a constructive perspective, however, it is sometimes more natural to think of functions as algorithms, or computer programs, that are presented in some explicit way. It is certainly the case that two computer programs can compute the same answer for every input despite the fact that they are syntactically quite different. In much the same way, you might want to maintain a view of functions that does not force you to identify two functions that have the same input / output behavior. This is known as an “intensional” view of functions.
In fact, function extensionality follows from the existence of quotients, which we describe in the next section. In the Lean standard library, therefore, `[funext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Functions/#funext "Documentation for funext in Lean Language Reference")` is thus [proved from the quotient construction](https://github.com/leanprover/lean4/blob/master/src/Init/Core.lean).
Suppose that for `α : Type u` we define the `Set ``α``:= α → Prop` to denote the type of subsets of `α`, essentially identifying subsets with predicates. By combining `[funext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Functions/#funext "Documentation for funext in Lean Language Reference")` and `[propext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Propositions/#propext "Documentation for propext in Lean Language Reference")`, we obtain an extensional theory of such sets:
`def Set (α : Type u) := α → Prop  ``[namespace](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___namespace "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Set  ``def mem (x : α) (a : Set α) := a x  ``infix:50 (priority := [high](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Instance-Declarations/#prioHigh "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference")) "∈" => [mem](Axioms-and-Computation/#Set-next "Definition of example")  ``theorem setext {a b : Set α} (h : ∀ x, x ∈ a ↔ x ∈ b) : a = b :=   [funext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Functions/#funext "Documentation for funext in Lean Language Reference") (fun x => [propext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Propositions/#propext "Documentation for propext in Lean Language Reference") (h x))  ``end Set ```
We can then proceed to define the empty set and set intersection, for example, and prove set identities:
`def Set (α : Type u) := α → Prop [namespace](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___namespace "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Set def mem (x : α) (a : Set α) := a x infix:50 (priority := [high](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Instance-Declarations/#prioHigh "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference")) "∈" => [mem](Axioms-and-Computation/#Set-next "Definition of example") theorem setext {a b : Set α} (h : ∀ x, x ∈ a ↔ x ∈ b) : a = b :=   [funext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Functions/#funext "Documentation for funext in Lean Language Reference") (fun x => [propext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Propositions/#propext "Documentation for propext in Lean Language Reference") (h x)) `
`def empty : Set α := fun _ => [False](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#False "Documentation for False in Lean Language Reference")  ``[notation](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Notations-and-Macros/Notations/#Lean___Parser___Command___notation "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") (priority := [False.elim](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#False___elim "Documentation for False.elim in Lean Language Reference") h)  ``theorem empty_inter (a : Set α) : ∅ ∩ a `end Set `
The following is an example of how function extensionality blocks computation inside the Lean kernel:
`def f (x : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")) := x ``def g (x : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")) := 0 + x  ``theorem f_eq_g : f = g :=   [funext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Functions/#funext "Documentation for funext in Lean Language Reference") fun x => (Nat.zero_add x).[symm](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___symm "Documentation for Eq.symm in Lean Language Reference")  ``def val : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference") :=   Eq.recOn (motive := fun _ _ => [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")) [f_eq_g](Axioms-and-Computation/#f-next-next-next-next-next-next-next-next-next-next "Definition of example") 0  ``-- does not reduce to 0 `[f_eq_g](Axioms-and-Computation/#f-next-next-next-next-next-next-next-next-next-next "Definition of example") ▸ 0`[#reduce](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Interacting-with-Lean/#Lean___reduceCmd "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") val`
```
[f_eq_g](Axioms-and-Computation/#f-next-next-next-next-next-next-next-next-next-next "Definition of example") ▸ 0
```
` ``-- evaluates to 0 `0`[#eval](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Interacting-with-Lean/#Lean___Parser___Command___eval "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") val`
```
0
```
``
First, we show that the two functions `f` and `g` are equal using function extensionality, and then we cast `0` of type `[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")` by replacing `f` by `g` in the type. Of course, the cast is vacuous, because `[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")` does not depend on `f`. But that is enough to do the damage: under the computational rules of the system, we now have a closed term of `[Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")` that does not reduce to a numeral. In this case, we may be tempted to reduce the expression to `0`. But in nontrivial examples, eliminating cast changes the type of the term, which might make an ambient expression type incorrect. The virtual machine, however, has no trouble evaluating the expression to `0`. Here is a similarly contrived example that shows how `[propext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Propositions/#propext "Documentation for propext in Lean Language Reference")` can get in the way:
`theorem tteq : ([True](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#True___intro "Documentation for True in Lean Language Reference") ∧ [True](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#True___intro "Documentation for True in Lean Language Reference")) = [True](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#True___intro "Documentation for True in Lean Language Reference") :=   [propext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Propositions/#propext "Documentation for propext in Lean Language Reference") ([Iff.intro](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Iff___intro "Documentation for Iff.intro in Lean Language Reference") (fun ⟨h, _⟩ => h) (fun h => ⟨h, h⟩))  ``def val : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference") :=   Eq.recOn (motive := fun _ _ => [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")) [tteq](Axioms-and-Computation/#tteq "Definition of example") 0  ``-- does not reduce to 0 `[tteq](Axioms-and-Computation/#tteq "Definition of example") ▸ 0`[#reduce](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Interacting-with-Lean/#Lean___reduceCmd "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") val`
```
[tteq](Axioms-and-Computation/#tteq "Definition of example") ▸ 0
```
` ``-- evaluates to 0 `0`[#eval](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Interacting-with-Lean/#Lean___Parser___Command___eval "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") val`
```
0
```
``
Current research programs, including work on _observational type theory_ and _cubical type theory_ , aim to extend type theory in ways that permit reductions for casts involving function extensionality, quotients, and more. But the solutions are not so clear-cut, and the rules of Lean's underlying calculus do not sanction such reductions.
In a sense, however, a cast does not change the meaning of an expression. Rather, it is a mechanism to reason about the expression's type. Given an appropriate semantics, it then makes sense to reduce terms in ways that preserve their meaning, ignoring the intermediate bookkeeping needed to make the reductions type-correct. In that case, adding new axioms in `Prop` does not matter; by [proof irrelevance](Propositions-and-Proofs/#--tech-term-proof-irrelevance), an expression in `Prop` carries no information, and can be safely ignored by the reduction procedures.
##  12.4. Quotients[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=quotients "Permalink")
Let `α` be any type, and let `r` be an equivalence relation on `α`. It is mathematically common to form the “quotient” `α / r`, that is, the type of elements of `α` “modulo” `r`. Set theoretically, one can view `α / r` as the set of equivalence classes of `α` modulo `r`. If `f : α → β` is any function that respects the equivalence relation in the sense that for every `x y : α`, `r x y` implies `f x = f y`, then `f` “lifts” to a function `f' : α / r → β` defined on each equivalence class `⟦x⟧` by `f' ⟦x⟧ = f x`. Lean's standard library extends the Calculus of Constructions with additional constants that perform exactly these constructions, and installs this last equation as a definitional reduction rule.
In its most basic form, the quotient construction does not even require `r` to be an equivalence relation. The following constants are built into Lean:
`[universe](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Universes/#Lean___Parser___Command___universe-next "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") u v  ``[axiom](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Axioms/#Lean___Parser___Command___axiom-next "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Quot : {α : Sort u} → (α → α → Prop) → Sort u  ``[axiom](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Axioms/#Lean___Parser___Command___axiom-next "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Quot.mk : {α : Sort u} → (r : α → α → Prop) → α → [Quot](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___Quot "Definition of example") r  ``[axiom](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Axioms/#Lean___Parser___Command___axiom-next "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Quot.ind :     ∀ {α : Sort u} {r : α → α → Prop} {β : [Quot](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___Quot "Definition of example") r → Prop},       (∀ a, β ([Quot.mk](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___Quot "Definition of example") r a)) → (q : [Quot](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___Quot "Definition of example") r) → β q  ``[axiom](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Axioms/#Lean___Parser___Command___axiom-next "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Quot.lift :     {α : Sort u} → {r : α → α → Prop} → {β : Sort u} → (f : α → β)     → (∀ a b, r a b → f a = f b) → [Quot](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___Quot "Definition of example") r → β `
The first one forms a type `[Quot](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot "Documentation for Quot in Lean Language Reference") r` given a type `α` by any binary relation `r` on `α`. The second maps `α` to `Quot α`, so that if `r : α → α → Prop` and `a : α`, then `[Quot.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") r a` is an element of `[Quot](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot "Documentation for Quot in Lean Language Reference") r`. The third principle, `[Quot.ind](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___ind "Documentation for Quot.ind in Lean Language Reference")`, says that every element of `[Quot.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") r a` is of this form. As for `[Quot.lift](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___lift "Documentation for Quot.lift in Lean Language Reference")`, given a function `f : α → β`, if `h` is a proof that `f` respects the relation `r`, then `[Quot.lift](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___lift "Documentation for Quot.lift in Lean Language Reference") f h` is the corresponding function on `[Quot](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot "Documentation for Quot in Lean Language Reference") r`. The idea is that for each element `a` in `α`, the function `[Quot.lift](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___lift "Documentation for Quot.lift in Lean Language Reference") f h` maps `[Quot.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") r a` (the `r`-class containing `a`) to `f a`, wherein `h` shows that this function is well defined. In fact, the computation principle is declared as a reduction rule, as the proof below makes clear.
`def mod7Rel (x y : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")) : Prop :=   x % 7 = y % 7  ``-- the quotient type `[Quot](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot "Documentation for Quot in Lean Language Reference") [mod7Rel](Axioms-and-Computation/#mod7Rel "Definition of example") : Type`[#check](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Interacting-with-Lean/#Lean___Parser___Command___check "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") ([Quot](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot "Documentation for Quot in Lean Language Reference") [mod7Rel](Axioms-and-Computation/#mod7Rel "Definition of example") : Type)`
```

` ``-- the computation principle example (a : [Nat](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Natural-Numbers/#Nat___zero "Documentation for Nat in Lean Language Reference")) : [Quot.lift](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___lift "Documentation for Quot.lift in Lean Language Reference") f [f_respects](Axioms-and-Computation/#mod7Rel "Definition of example") ([Quot.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") [mod7Rel](Axioms-and-Computation/#mod7Rel "Definition of example") a) = f a :=   rfl ```
The four constants, `[Quot](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot "Documentation for Quot in Lean Language Reference")`, `[Quot.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference")`, `[Quot.ind](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___ind "Documentation for Quot.ind in Lean Language Reference")`, and `[Quot.lift](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___lift "Documentation for Quot.lift in Lean Language Reference")` in and of themselves are not very strong. You can check that the `[Quot.ind](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___ind "Documentation for Quot.ind in Lean Language Reference")` is satisfied if we take `[Quot](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot "Documentation for Quot in Lean Language Reference") r` to be simply `α`, and take `[Quot.lift](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___lift "Documentation for Quot.lift in Lean Language Reference")` to be the identity function (ignoring `h`). For that reason, these four constants are not viewed as additional axioms.
They are, like inductively defined types and the associated constructors and recursors, viewed as part of the logical framework.
What makes the `[Quot](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot "Documentation for Quot in Lean Language Reference")` construction into a bona fide quotient is the following additional axiom:
`[axiom](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Axioms/#Lean___Parser___Command___axiom-next "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Quot.sound :       ∀ {α : Type u} {r : α → α → Prop} {a b : α},         r a b → [Quot.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") r a = [Quot.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") r b ```
This is the axiom that asserts that any two elements of `α` that are related by `r` become identified in the quotient. If a theorem or definition makes use of `Quot.sound`, it will show up in the `#print axioms` command.
Of course, the quotient construction is most commonly used in situations when `r` is an equivalence relation. Given `r` as above, if we define `r'` according to the rule `r' a b` iff `[Quot.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") r a = [Quot.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") r b`, then it's clear that `r'` is an equivalence relation. Indeed, `r'` is the _kernel_ of the function `fun a => [Quot.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") r a`. The axiom `[Quot.sound](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___sound "Documentation for Quot.sound in Lean Language Reference")` says that `r a b` implies `r' a b`. Using `[Quot.lift](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___lift "Documentation for Quot.lift in Lean Language Reference")` and `[Quot.ind](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___ind "Documentation for Quot.ind in Lean Language Reference")`, we can show that `r'` is the smallest equivalence relation containing `r`, in the sense that if `r''` is any equivalence relation containing `r`, then `r' a b` implies `r'' a b`. In particular, if `r` was an equivalence relation to start with, then for all `a` and `b` we have `r a b` iff `r' a b`.
To support this common use case, the standard library defines the notion of a _setoid_ , which is simply a type with an associated equivalence relation:
`class Setoid (α : Sort u) where   r : α → α → Prop   iseqv : [Equivalence](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Equivalence___mk "Documentation for Equivalence in Lean Language Reference") r  ``instance {α : Sort u} [[Setoid](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___Setoid "Definition of example") α] : [HasEquiv](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#HasEquiv___mk "Documentation for HasEquiv in Lean Language Reference") α :=   ⟨[Setoid.r](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___Setoid "Definition of example")⟩  ``[namespace](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___namespace "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Setoid  ``[variable](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___variable "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") {α : Sort u} [[Setoid](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___Setoid "Definition of example") α]  ``theorem refl (a : α) : a ≈ a :=   [iseqv](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___Setoid "Definition of example").[refl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Equivalence___mk "Documentation for Equivalence.refl in Lean Language Reference") a  ``theorem symm {a b : α} (hab : a ≈ b) : b ≈ a :=   [iseqv](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___Setoid "Definition of example").[symm](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Equivalence___mk "Documentation for Equivalence.symm in Lean Language Reference") hab  ``theorem trans {a b c : α} (hab : a ≈ b) (hbc : b ≈ c) : a ≈ c :=   [iseqv](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___Setoid "Definition of example").[trans](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Equivalence___mk "Documentation for Equivalence.trans in Lean Language Reference") hab hbc  ``end Setoid `
Given a type `α`, a relation `r` on `α`, and a proof `iseqv` that `r` is an equivalence relation, we can define an instance of the `Setoid` class.
`def Quotient {α : Sort u} (s : [Setoid](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Setoid___mk "Documentation for Setoid in Lean Language Reference") α) :=   @[Quot](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot "Documentation for Quot in Lean Language Reference") α Setoid.r `
The constants `[Quotient.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient___mk "Documentation for Quotient.mk in Lean Language Reference")`, `[Quotient.ind](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient___ind "Documentation for Quotient.ind in Lean Language Reference")`, `[Quotient.lift](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient___lift "Documentation for Quotient.lift in Lean Language Reference")`, and `[Quotient.sound](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient___sound "Documentation for Quotient.sound in Lean Language Reference")` are nothing more than the specializations of the corresponding elements of `[Quot](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot "Documentation for Quot in Lean Language Reference")`. The fact that type class inference can find the setoid associated to a type `α` brings a number of benefits. First, we can use the notation `a ≈ b` (entered with `\approx`) for `[Setoid.r](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Setoid___mk "Documentation for Setoid.r in Lean Language Reference") a b`, where the instance of `[Setoid](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Setoid___mk "Documentation for Setoid in Lean Language Reference")` is implicit in the notation `[Setoid.r](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Setoid___mk "Documentation for Setoid.r in Lean Language Reference")`. We can use the generic theorems `[Setoid.refl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Setoid___refl "Documentation for Setoid.refl in Lean Language Reference")`, `[Setoid.symm](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Setoid___symm "Documentation for Setoid.symm in Lean Language Reference")`, `[Setoid.trans](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Setoid___trans "Documentation for Setoid.trans in Lean Language Reference")` to reason about the relation. Specifically with quotients we can use the theorem `[Quotient.exact](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient___exact "Documentation for Quotient.exact in Lean Language Reference")`:
`[Quotient.exact](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient___exact "Documentation for Quotient.exact in Lean Language Reference") {α : Sort u} {s : [Setoid](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Setoid___mk "Documentation for Setoid in Lean Language Reference") α} {a b : α} :   [Quotient.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient___mk "Documentation for Quotient.mk in Lean Language Reference") s a = [Quotient.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient___mk "Documentation for Quotient.mk in Lean Language Reference") s b →   a ≈ b `
Together with `[Quotient.sound](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient___sound "Documentation for Quotient.sound in Lean Language Reference")`, this implies that the elements of the quotient correspond exactly to the equivalence classes of elements in `α`.
Recall that in the standard library, `α × β` represents the Cartesian product of the types `α` and `β`. To illustrate the use of quotients, let us define the type of _unordered_ pairs of elements of a type `α` as a quotient of the type `α × α`. First, we define the relevant equivalence relation:
`private def eqv (p₁ p₂ : α × α) : Prop :=   (p₁.1 = p₂.1 ∧ p₁.2 = p₂.2) ∨ (p₁.1 = p₂.2 ∧ p₁.2 = p₂.1)  ``infix:50 " ~ " => eqv ```
The next step is to prove that `eqv` is in fact an equivalence relation, which is to say, it is reflexive, symmetric and transitive. We can prove these three facts in a convenient and readable way by using dependent pattern matching to perform case-analysis and break the hypotheses into pieces that are then reassembled to produce the conclusion.
`private def eqv (p₁ p₂ : α × α) : Prop :=   (p₁.1 = p₂.1 ∧ p₁.2 = p₂.2) ∨ (p₁.1 = p₂.2 ∧ p₁.2 = p₂.1) infix:50 " ~ " => [eqv](Axioms-and-Computation/#_private___0___eqv "Definition of example") `
`private theorem eqv.refl (p : α × α) : p ~ p :=   [Or.inl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or.inl in Lean Language Reference") ⟨[rfl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#rfl-next "Documentation for rfl in Lean Language Reference"), [rfl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#rfl-next "Documentation for rfl in Lean Language Reference")⟩  ``private theorem eqv.symm : ∀ {p₁ p₂ : α × α}, p₁ ~ p₂ → p₂ ~ p₁   | (a₁, a₂), (b₁, b₂), ([Or.inl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or.inl in Lean Language Reference") ⟨a₁b₁, a₂b₂⟩) =>     [Or.inl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or.inl in Lean Language Reference") (byα:Type is_equivalence : [Equivalence](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Equivalence___mk "Documentation for Equivalence in Lean Language Reference") (@[eqv](Axioms-and-Computation/#_private___0___eqv "Definition of example") α) :=   { [refl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Equivalence___mk "Documentation for Equivalence.refl in Lean Language Reference") := [eqv.refl](Axioms-and-Computation/#_private___0___eqv___refl "Definition of example"), [symm](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Equivalence___mk "Documentation for Equivalence.symm in Lean Language Reference") := [eqv.symm](Axioms-and-Computation/#_private___0___eqv___refl "Definition of example"), [trans](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Equivalence___mk "Documentation for Equivalence.trans in Lean Language Reference") := [eqv.trans](Axioms-and-Computation/#_private___0___eqv___refl "Definition of example") } ```
Now that we have proved that `[eqv](Axioms-and-Computation/#_private___0___eqv "Definition of example")` is an equivalence relation, we can construct a `[Setoid](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Setoid___mk "Documentation for Setoid in Lean Language Reference") (α × α)`, and use it to define the type `[UProd](Axioms-and-Computation/#uprodSetoid "Definition of example") α` of unordered pairs.
`private def eqv (p₁ p₂ : α × α) : Prop :=   (p₁.1 = p₂.1 ∧ p₁.2 = p₂.2) ∨ (p₁.1 = p₂.2 ∧ p₁.2 = p₂.1) infix:50 " ~ " => [eqv](Axioms-and-Computation/#_private___0___eqv "Definition of example") private theorem eqv.refl (p : α × α) : p ~ p :=   [Or.inl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or.inl in Lean Language Reference") ⟨[rfl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#rfl-next "Documentation for rfl in Lean Language Reference"), [rfl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#rfl-next "Documentation for rfl in Lean Language Reference")⟩ private theorem eqv.symm : ∀ {p₁ p₂ : α × α}, p₁ ~ p₂ → p₂ ~ p₁   | (a₁, a₂), (b₁, b₂), ([Or.inl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or.inl in Lean Language Reference") `instance uprodSetoid (α : Type u) : [Setoid](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Setoid___mk "Documentation for Setoid in Lean Language Reference") (α × α) where   [r](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Setoid___mk "Documentation for Setoid.r in Lean Language Reference")     := [eqv](Axioms-and-Computation/#_private___0___eqv "Definition of example")   [iseqv](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Setoid___mk "Documentation for Setoid.iseqv in Lean Language Reference") := [is_equivalence](Axioms-and-Computation/#_private___0___eqv___refl "Definition of example")  ``def UProd (α : Type u) : Type u :=   [Quotient](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient "Documentation for Quotient in Lean Language Reference") ([uprodSetoid](Axioms-and-Computation/#uprodSetoid "Definition of example") α)  ``[namespace](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___namespace "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") UProd  ``def mk {α : Type} (a₁ a₂ : α) : [UProd](Axioms-and-Computation/#uprodSetoid "Definition of example") α :=   [Quotient.mk'](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient___mk___ "Documentation for Quotient.mk' in Lean Language Reference") (a₁, a₂)  ``[notation](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Notations-and-Macros/Notations/#Lean___Parser___Command___notation "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") "{ " a₁ ", " a₂ " }" => [mk](Axioms-and-Computation/#uprodSetoid "Definition of example") a₁ a₂  ``end UProd ```
Notice that we locally define the notation `{a₁, a₂}` for unordered pairs as `[Quotient.mk'](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient___mk___ "Documentation for Quotient.mk' in Lean Language Reference") (a₁, a₂)`. This is useful for illustrative purposes, but it is not a good idea in general, since the notation will shadow other uses of curly brackets, such as for records and sets.
We can easily prove that `{a₁, a₂} = {a₂, a₁}` using `[Quot.sound](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___sound "Documentation for Quot.sound in Lean Language Reference")`, since we have `(a₁, a₂) ~ (a₂, a₁)`.
`private def eqv (p₁ p₂ : α × α) : Prop :=   (p₁.1 = p₂.1 ∧ p₁.2 = p₂.2) ∨ (p₁.1 = p₂.2 ∧ p₁.2 = p₂.1) infix:50 " ~ " => [eqv](Axioms-and-Computation/#_private___0___eqv "Definition of example") `theorem mk_eq_mk (a₁ a₂ : α) : {a₁, a₂} = {a₂, a₁} :=   [Quot.sound](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___sound "Documentation for Quot.sound in Lean Language Reference") ([Or.inr](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or.inr in Lean Language Reference") ⟨[rfl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#rfl-next "Documentation for rfl in Lean Language Reference"), [rfl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#rfl-next "Documentation for rfl in Lean Language Reference")⟩) `
`end UProd `
To complete the example, given `a : α` and `u : [UProd](Axioms-and-Computation/#uprodSetoid "Definition of example") α`, we define the proposition `a`` ∈ ``u` which should hold if `a` is one of the elements of the unordered pair `u`. First, we define a similar proposition `mem_fn``a``u` on (ordered) pairs; then we show that `mem_fn` respects the equivalence relation `[eqv](Axioms-and-Computation/#_private___0___eqv "Definition of example")` with the lemma `mem_respects`. This is an idiom that is used extensively in the Lean standard library.
`set_option linter.unusedVariables false private def eqv (p₁ p₂ : α × α) : Prop :=   (p₁.1 = p₂.1 ∧ p₁.2 = p₂.2) ∨ (p₁.1 = p₂.2 ∧ p₁.2 = p₂.1) infix:50 " ~ " => `end UProd `
For convenience, the standard library also defines `[Quotient.lift₂](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quotient___lift___ "Documentation for Quotient.lift₂ in Lean Language Reference")` for lifting binary functions, and `Quotient.ind₂` for induction on two variables.
We close this section with some hints as to why the quotient construction implies function extensionality. It is not hard to show that extensional equality on the `(x : α) → β x` is an equivalence relation, and so we can consider the type `extfun α β` of functions “up to equivalence.” Of course, application respects that equivalence in the sense that if `f₁` is equivalent to `f₂`, then `f₁ a` is equal to `f₂ a`. Thus application gives rise to a function `extfun_app : extfun α β → (x : α) → β x`. But for every `f`, `extfun_app ([.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") _ f)` is definitionally equal to `fun x => f x`, which is in turn definitionally equal to `f`. So, when `f₁` and `f₂` are extensionally equal, we have the following chain of equalities:
`[variable](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___variable "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") {α : Sort u} {β : α → Sort v}  def extfun (α : Sort u) (β : α → Sort v) := [Quot](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot "Documentation for Quot in Lean Language Reference") (fun (f g : (x : α) → β x) => ∀ x, f x = g x)  def extfun_app {α β} (f : extfun α β) (x : α) : β x :=   [Quot.lift](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___lift "Documentation for Quot.lift in Lean Language Reference") (· x) (byα✝:Sort uβ✝:α✝ → Sort vα:Sort ?u.75β:α → Sort ?u.74f:extfun α βx:α⊢ ∀ (a b : (x : α) → β x), (∀ (x : α), a x [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") b x) → (fun x_1 => x_1 x) a [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") (fun x_1 => x_1 x) b [intros](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#intros "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference")α✝:Sort uβ✝:α✝ → Sort vα:Sort ?u.75β:α → Sort ?u.74f:extfun α βx:αa✝¹:(x : α) → β xb✝:(x : α) → β xa✝:∀ (x : α), a✝¹ x [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") b✝ x⊢ (fun x_1 => x_1 x) a✝¹ [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") (fun x_1 => x_1 x) b✝; [simp](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#simp "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference") [*]All goals completed! 🐙) f `
`example (f₁ f₂ : (x : α) → β x) (h : ∀ x, f₁ x = f₂ x) :=   calc f₁     _ = extfun_app ([.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") _ f₁) := [rfl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#rfl-next "Documentation for rfl in Lean Language Reference")     _ = extfun_app ([.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") _ f₂) := byα:Sort uβ:α → Sort vf₁:(x : α) → β xf₂:(x : α) → β xh:∀ (x : α), f₁ x [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") f₂ x⊢ extfun_app ([Quot.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") (fun f g => ∀ (x : α), f x [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") g x) f₁) [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") extfun_app ([Quot.mk](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___mk "Documentation for Quot.mk in Lean Language Reference") (fun f g => ∀ (x : α), f x [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") g x) f₂) [rw](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#rw "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference") [[Quot.sound](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Quotients/#Quot___sound "Documentation for Quot.sound in Lean Language Reference")]α:Sort uβ:α → Sort vf₁:(x : α) → β xf₂:(x : α) → β xh:∀ (x : α), f₁ x [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") f₂ x⊢ ∀ (x : α), f₁ x [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") f₂ x; [trivial](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/Tactic-Reference/#trivial "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference")All goals completed! 🐙     _ = f₂ := rfl  ```
As a result, `f₁` is equal to `f₂`.
##  12.5. Choice[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=choice "Permalink")
To state the final axiom defined in the standard library, we need the `Nonempty` type, which is defined as follows:
`class inductive Nonempty (α : Sort u) : Prop where   | intro (val : α) : [Nonempty](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___Nonempty "Definition of example") α `
Because `[Nonempty](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Nonempty___intro "Documentation for Nonempty in Lean Language Reference") α` has type `Prop` and its constructor contains data, it can only eliminate to `Prop`. In fact, `[Nonempty](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Nonempty___intro "Documentation for Nonempty in Lean Language Reference") α` is equivalent to `∃ x : α, [True](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#True___intro "Documentation for True in Lean Language Reference")`:
`example (α : Type u) : [Nonempty](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Nonempty___intro "Documentation for Nonempty in Lean Language Reference") α ↔ ∃ `unused variable `x`  Note: This linter can be disabled with `set_option linter.unusedVariables false``x : α, [True](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#True___intro "Documentation for True in Lean Language Reference") := [Iff.intro](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Iff___intro "Documentation for Iff.intro in Lean Language Reference") (fun ⟨a⟩ => ⟨a, trivial⟩) (fun ⟨a, `unused variable `h`  Note: This linter can be disabled with `set_option linter.unusedVariables false``h⟩ => ⟨a⟩) ```
Our axiom of choice is now expressed simply as follows:
`[axiom](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Axioms/#Lean___Parser___Command___axiom-next "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") choice {α : Sort u} : [Nonempty](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Nonempty___intro "Documentation for Nonempty in Lean Language Reference") α → α `
Given only the assertion `h` that `α` is nonempty, `choice h` magically produces an element of `α`. Of course, this blocks any meaningful computation: by the interpretation of `Prop`, `h` contains no information at all as to how to find such an element.
This is found in the `Classical` namespace, so the full name of the theorem is `Classical.choice`. The choice principle is equivalent to the principle of **indefinite description** , which can be expressed with subtypes as follows:
`noncomputable def indefiniteDescription {α : Sort u}     (p : α → Prop) (h : ∃ x, p x) : {x // p x} :=   [choice](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___choice "Definition of example") <| let ⟨x, px⟩ := h; ⟨⟨x, px⟩⟩ `
Because it depends on `choice`, Lean cannot generate executable code for `indefiniteDescription`, and so requires us to mark the definition as `noncomputable`. Also in the `Classical` namespace, the function `choose` and the property `choose_spec` decompose the two parts of the output of `indefiniteDescription`:
`[variable](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___variable "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") {α : Sort u} {p : α → Prop}  ``noncomputable def choose (h : ∃ x, p x) : α :=   (indefiniteDescription p h).[val](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Types/Subtypes/#Subtype___mk "Documentation for Subtype.val in Lean Language Reference")  ``theorem choose_spec (h : ∃ x, p x) : p ([choose](Axioms-and-Computation/#Hidden___choose "Definition of example") h) :=   (indefiniteDescription p h).property `
The `choice` principle also erases the distinction between the property of being `[Nonempty](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Nonempty___intro "Documentation for Nonempty in Lean Language Reference")` and the more constructive property of being `[Inhabited](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Inhabited___mk "Documentation for Inhabited in Lean Language Reference")`:
`[open](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___open "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Classical `
`noncomputable def inhabited_of_nonempty (h : [Nonempty](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Nonempty___intro "Documentation for Nonempty in Lean Language Reference") α) : [Inhabited](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Inhabited___mk "Documentation for Inhabited in Lean Language Reference") α :=   choice (let ⟨a⟩ := h; ⟨⟨a⟩⟩) ```
In the next section, we will see that `[propext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Propositions/#propext "Documentation for propext in Lean Language Reference")`, `[funext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Functions/#funext "Documentation for funext in Lean Language Reference")`, and `choice`, taken together, imply the law of the excluded middle and the decidability of all propositions. Using those, one can strengthen the principle of indefinite description as follows:
`strongIndefiniteDescription {α : Sort u} (p : α → Prop)   (h : [Nonempty](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Nonempty___intro "Documentation for Nonempty in Lean Language Reference") α) :   {x // (∃ (y : α), p y) → p x} `
Assuming the ambient type `α` is nonempty, `strongIndefiniteDescription`` ``p` produces an element of `α` satisfying `p` if there is one. The data component of this definition is conventionally known as **Hilbert's epsilon function** :
`epsilon {α : Sort u} [h : [Nonempty](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Nonempty___intro "Documentation for Nonempty in Lean Language Reference") α] (p : α → Prop) : α `
`epsilon_spec {α : Sort u} {p : α → Prop}   (hex : ∃ (y : α), p y) :   p (@epsilon _ (nonempty_of_exists hex) p) `
##  12.6. The Law of the Excluded Middle[🔗](find/?domain=Verso.Genre.Manual.section&name=the-law-of-the-excluded-middle "Permalink")
The law of the excluded middle is the following:
`Classical.em : ∀ (p : Prop), p ∨ ¬p `
[Diaconescu's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diaconescu%27s_theorem) states that the axiom of choice is sufficient to derive the law of excluded middle. More precisely, it shows that the law of the excluded middle follows from `Classical.choice`, `[propext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Propositions/#propext "Documentation for propext in Lean Language Reference")`, and `[funext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Functions/#funext "Documentation for funext in Lean Language Reference")`. We sketch the proof that is found in the standard library.
First, we import the necessary axioms, and define two predicates `U` and `V`:
`[open](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___open "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Classical theorem em (p : Prop) : p ∨ ¬p := byp:Prop⊢ p [∨](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or in Lean Language Reference") [¬](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Not "Documentation for Not in Lean Language Reference")p If `p` is true, then every element of `Prop` is in both `U` and `V`. If `p` is false, then `U` is the singleton `[True](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#True___intro "Documentation for True in Lean Language Reference")`, and `V` is the singleton `[False](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#False "Documentation for False in Lean Language Reference")`.
Next, we use `choose` to choose an element from each of `U` and `V`:
`[let](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Tactic-Proofs/The-Tactic-Language/#let "Documentation for tactic in Lean Language Reference") u : Prop := choose exUp:PropU:Prop → Prop := fun x => x [∨](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or in Lean Language Reference") pV:Prop → Prop := fun x => x [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") [False](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#False "Documentation for False in Lean Language Reference") [∨](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or in Lean Language Reference") pexU:[∃](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Quantifiers/#Exists___intro "Documentation for Exists in Lean Language Reference") [Exists.intro](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Quantifiers/#Exists___intro "Documentation for Exists.intro in Lean Language Reference") [True](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#True___intro "Documentation for True in Lean Language Reference") ([Or.inl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or.inl in Lean Language Reference") Each of `U` and `V` is a disjunction, so `u_def` and `v_def` represent four cases. In one of these cases, `u [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") [True](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#True___intro "Documentation for True in Lean Language Reference")` and `v [=](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#Eq___refl "Documentation for Eq in Lean Language Reference") [False](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#False "Documentation for False in Lean Language Reference")`, and in all the other cases, `p` is true. Thus we have:
`have not_uv_or_p : u ≠ v ∨ p := byp:Prop⊢ p [∨](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or in Lean Language Reference") completed! 🐙 `
On the other hand, if `p` is true, then, by function extensionality and propositional extensionality, `U` and `V` are equal. By the definition of `u` and `v`, this implies that they are equal as well.

Consequences of excluded middle include double-negation elimination, proof by cases, and proof by contradiction, all of which are described in the section on [classical logic](Propositions-and-Proofs/#classical-logic). The law of the excluded middle and propositional extensionality imply propositional completeness:
`[open](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___open "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Classical ``theorem propComplete (a : Prop) : a = [True](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#True___intro "Documentation for True in Lean Language Reference") ∨ a = [False](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#False "Documentation for False in Lean Language Reference") :=   [match](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Pattern-Matching/#Lean___Parser___Term___match "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") em a [with](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Pattern-Matching/#Lean___Parser___Term___match "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference")   | [Or.inl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or.inl in Lean Language Reference") ha =>     [Or.inl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or.inl in Lean Language Reference") ([propext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Propositions/#propext "Documentation for propext in Lean Language Reference") ([Iff.intro](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Iff___intro "Documentation for Iff.intro in Lean Language Reference") (fun _ => [True.intro](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#True___intro "Documentation for True.intro in Lean Language Reference")) (fun _ => ha)))   | [Or.inr](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or.inr in Lean Language Reference") hn =>     [Or.inr](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Or___inl "Documentation for Or.inr in Lean Language Reference") ([propext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Propositions/#propext "Documentation for propext in Lean Language Reference") ([Iff.intro](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Logical-Connectives/#Iff___intro "Documentation for Iff.intro in Lean Language Reference") (fun h => hn h) (fun h => [False.elim](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Truth/#False___elim "Documentation for False.elim in Lean Language Reference") h))) ```
Together with choice, we also get the stronger principle that every proposition is decidable. Recall that the class of `[Decidable](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Decidable___isFalse "Documentation for Decidable in Lean Language Reference")` propositions is defined as follows:
`[namespace](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___namespace "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Hidden `
`class inductive Decidable (p : Prop) where   | isFalse (h : ¬p) : Decidable p   | isTrue  (h : p)  : Decidable p `
`end Hidden `
In contrast to `p ∨ ¬ p`, which can only eliminate to `Prop`, the type `[Decidable](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Decidable___isFalse "Documentation for Decidable in Lean Language Reference") p` is equivalent to the sum type `Sum p (¬ p)`, which can eliminate to any type. It is this data that is needed to write an if-then-else expression.
As an example of classical reasoning, we use `choose` to show that if `f : α → β` is injective and `α` is inhabited, then `f` has a left inverse. To define the left inverse `linv`, we use a dependent if-then-else expression. Recall that `[if](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") h : c [then](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") t [else](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") e` is notation for `dite c (fun h : c => t) (fun h : ¬ c => e)`. In the definition of `linv`, choice is used twice: first, to show that `(∃ a : α, f a = b)` is “decidable,” and then to choose an `a` such that `f a = b`. Notice that `propDecidable` is a scoped instance and is activated by the `[open](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___open "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Classical` command. We use this instance to justify the `if`-`then`-`else` expression. (See also the discussion in [Decidable Propositions](Type-Classes/#decidable-propositions)).
`[open](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Namespaces-and-Sections/#Lean___Parser___Command___open "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") Classical  ``noncomputable def linv [[Inhabited](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Inhabited___mk "Documentation for Inhabited in Lean Language Reference") α] (f : α → β) : β → α :=   fun b : β => [if](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") ex : (∃ a : α, f a = b) [then](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") choose ex [else](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Terms/Conditionals/#termDepIfThenElse "Documentation for syntax in Lean Language Reference") [default](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Inhabited___mk "Documentation for Inhabited.default in Lean Language Reference")  ``theorem linv_comp_self {f : α → β} [[Inhabited](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Type-Classes/Basic-Classes/#Inhabited___mk "Documentation for Inhabited in Lean Language Reference") α]                        (inj : ∀ {a b}, f a = f b → a = b)                        : [linv](Axioms-and-Computation/#linv "Definition of example") f ∘ f = id :=   [funext](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/The-Type-System/Functions/#funext "Documentation for funext in Lean Language Reference") fun a =>     have ex  : ∃ a₁ : α, f a₁ = f a := ⟨a, [rfl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#rfl-next "Documentation for rfl in Lean Language Reference")⟩     have feq : f (choose ex) = f a  := choose_spec ex     calc [linv](Axioms-and-Computation/#linv "Definition of example") f (f a)       _ = choose ex := [rfl](https://lean-lang.org/doc/reference/4.26.0/Basic-Propositions/Propositional-Equality/#rfl-next "Documentation for rfl in Lean Language Reference")       _ = a         := inj feq ```
From a classical point of view, `linv` is a function. From a constructive point of view, it is unacceptable; because there is no way to implement such a function in general, the construction is not informative.
